TO AVOID CONTENTIONS.
WHILE the success of the Republicans in this
campaign scenis well assured throughout the
there are nevertheless abundant .reasons
why every effort should be made to preserve harmony
iv the ranks so that the full party vote will be given
to every candidate. It will certainly be an act m
folly for those having authority to adopt a course
which will give rise to contentions or even to an ex
cuse for them. 11l feeling engendered by any act of
party leaders is apt to rankle and spread, and prove a
serious weakness to the organization as a whole.
At the present time there exist differences of
opinion concerning the expediency of holding pri- j
:nary elections in some Congressional districts for j
the purpose of electing delegates to a convention to
choose district delegates to the Republican National
Convention. Where such differences exist the dis
trict committees should call primaries. Any other
course will not only augment the discord in the party,
but will give ground on which contentions can be
bai-ed and sustained before the national convention.
The issue is a plain one. The call of the National
Committee for the national convention says: "Th-
Congressional district delegates shall be elected by
conventions called by the Congressional Committee
of each district, in the manner of nominating candi
dates for Representative in Congress in said district."
Under the law of California it is provided: "All dele
gates to conventions of political parties for the pur
pose of making nominations for candidates for public
ofi";ce within this State shall be elected at elections to
be known and designated as primary elections and
conducted at the time and place and under the regu
lations in this chapter hereinafter specified, and not
otherwise.
There can be no doubt as to the intent of the pri
mary law, nor of the call of the National Committees
Hence primary elections are necessary in every dis
trict where there is the least objection to any other
course of action. In some districts it is already cer
tain an attempt to avoid primaries will lead to a fac
tion f:?ht in the party ranks and the election of a
contesting delegation to the national convention.
In the Fifth District, for example, a strong body of
Republicans "have given notice that they will not be
found by a convention composed of delegates not
elected at primaries, and that if such a convention be
appointed they will call a convention of their own
and hold primaries as the law requires. That will
mean n division of the party in that district at the very
outset of the campaign, the appearance of representa
tives of the contending factions before the National
Coir.rrittee, and a controversy which will engender
bad I^Dod.on both sides.
There are other districts where the situation is as
critic? i as in the Fifth. It is known that in Ala-.ned.i
County a considerable number of Republicans will
onieci to a convention made up of appointed instead
of elected delegate?, and a similar condition of affairs
prevr-Is elsewhere. It is not worth while, however,
to pro over the whole State for examples. The safe
rule is that which we have stated â wherever there is
any difference of sentiment on the subject it is the
duty of the party authorities to provide for primaries.
The members of the party who demand primaries
have on their side the call of the National Committee
ard the law of the State. An attempt, therefore, to
ijrnore their demand would be a blunder of the worst
kind, and might seriously compromise the campaign
ir. more ways than one.

Young District Messenger Conveying From Philadelphia an Album Containing Newspaper Clippings
Favoring the Cause of the Boers and a Sympathetic Message Signed by 28,000 Schoolboys.
JAMES FRANCIS SMITH. THE COURIER; MAP SHOWING HIS ROUTE; FAC SIMILE OF PORTION OF PAGE
IN THE ALBUM, AND THE NORTH AMERICAN'S ADDRESS TO "OOM" PAUL. .
A PASSENGER on the swift ocean liner St. Louis, plowing
her way across the Atlantic, is James Francis Smith, a 16
year-old American District messenger, who is carrying
to President Kruger at Pretoria a message of sympathy
signed by 25.000 school boys of Philadelphia. Accompanying
this message is an album nearly two feet square, containing 500
leaves, on which are pasted clippings from prominent papers
in the United States. Among these clippings is one from The
Call of March 14, containing Father Yorke's stirring plea for
the brave burghers.
The album is being sent to President Kruger by the North
American in order vhat the people of South Africa may learn
what the press of America has to say about them.
Although W. W. Rayons, the general superintendent of the
American District Telegraph Company in New York, declares
that any messenger in the service would carry a message to
President Kruger. as a matter of course, and would start
without a moment's hesitation, young Smith is the happy se
lection from nearly 2000 boys. When Mr. Rayons was asked to
"ring up a boy" he sent orders to the district managers to
have the pick of their boys report at his office. About forty
boys responded, and these were quickly weeded out until three
remained. They were kept under observation for a couple of
days and finally the selection fell on young Smith. He was de-
tailed to special duty under Mr. Rayons and had no idea of.
the important mission intended for h!m.
He remained under scrutiny for several days and was then
suddenly asked. "Will you carry a message to President
Kruger?"
"Yes. sir," came the instant reply.*
"Do you know -where to find him?"
"Yes. sir; in South Africa."
"Supposing you find that President Kramer has been taken
to St. Helena when you reach South Africa, what will you do'/"
"Follow him to St. Helena, or to the end of the earth if
necessary.". ;.-!
"Do you know where St. Helena is?"
"Yes* sir; it is the island where Napoleon was Imprisoned
The boy. although earnest and alert, showed no more sur
prise or emotion when told that he was to go on an errand of
nearly 12,000 miles than if he had been sent around the corner
with a local telegram.
When asked what he would do if the British soldiers at
tempted to stop him on his way to Pretoria, he replied. "I would
appeal to an American Consul for protection."
The boy. who is known on the messenger force as No. V 'i.
will reach Havre on the ISth inst. and will then follow the
route indicated on the above map to Pretoria.
A WESTERN MAN WANTED.
REPUBLICAN leaders in the East are reported
to be well nigh unanimous in desiring the
nomination of a Western candidate for the
Vice Presidency on the Republican ticket. The occa
sion is therefore. propitious for the advancement of
such a candidate. It appears certain that if the Great
West will put forward a strong man, and give him
cordial support, the nomination for that high office
can be obtained for him.
The opportunity thus afforded for California to
place one of her citizens in the Vice Presidency
should not be overlooked. This is the Empire State
of the West. Its prestige on the Pacific Coast is
higher than that of New York on the Atlantic, but
that prestige has never been exerted in politics, and
accordingly the State has never had anything like its
rightful power and place in governmental affairs. The
call for a Western man coming at this time is there
fore one to which the Republicans of California
should give heed. The nomination is equivalent to
an election, and by the display of harmony and
energy at this juncture we may mark the beginning
of the new century by installing in the second highest
office of the republic a representative of the Pacific
There are many reasons why a Western man should
be preferred to one from the East at this time. In
the first place the choice of a candidate would
strengthen the ticket to some extent in this section
of the Union, and in the second place it would be a
due recognition of the growing greatness of the West
as compared with that section of the Union lying cast
of the Mississippi. The time has gone by when Eastern
wits could with any show of reason refer to the com
monwealths of the West as "mining camps." The
mining industry remains, but to it has been added
every other form of industry, and population and
wealth have increased to such an extent that these
States have now a right to claim a full share of the
highest offices in the land.
California does not lack for citizens well fitted t:Â»
fill the Vice Presidential office. It would be invidious
to cite the names of some worthy of the office while
omitting others, but it would be easy to name several
whom the people of the State could support with a
just pride for the position. The Call has no par
ticular candidate of ifs own to urge upon the party.
It will cordially unite in supporting any man whom
the Republicans of the State will nominate. The issue
is before the party. There comes from the East what
appears to be an earnest call for a Western candi
date, and California should not lose the opportunity
to advance her political prestige by presenting a man
whose candidacy would be acceptable to Republicans
.throughout the Union.

AMERICAN WORDS OF CHEER FOR PRESIDENT KRUGER
THE SAN FRAJSTCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, 'APRIL 18, 1900.
THE COOLIE SHADOW
THERE is no room for any honest or thoughtful
person to misunderstand the purpose of The
Call's attempt to prevent the illegal landing ut
Chinese by exposing the method of it. In our first
expose we made no charge against any official. We
simply reproduced the official records, leaving con
clusions to be drawn by the reader. It is known that
not many Chinese laborers here have families; that
is one objection to their presence among us. Surely,
then, it is enough to put officials on their caution that
3000 male coolies are admitted within the last three
years as native sons, who went back to China before
the first exclusion law in 1882, and have concluded to
return to their birthplace only when the last law and
treaty close to them all methods of entry, except the
native son route. It will be interesting to examine
the proofs made of their birthplace and see the small
percentage born outside of San Francisco, and the
large proportion, running into the hundreds, who
were born at the same number on some street in
Chinatown. All of this internal evidence that the
majority of their landings are by fraud and perjury
is accessible.
What The Call desires is that the last treaty with
China and the exclusion law passed in pursuance
thereof be honestly administered. We desire that all
Chinese excepted from exclusion shall have their
rights, without -being compelled to bribe any Ameri
can officer to get them. We admit that proof may be
shown of cases in which Chinese merchants, and
others in the excepted classes, have been compelled
to pay dishonest officers for a right that belonged to
them under the law. We admit, also, that it is the
rule that when men have to secure by bribery what
belongs to them, they will recoup themselves by get
ting by bribery what does not belong to them. This
has been the curse of this whole Chinese business.
It has run through, it all. from the payment to the
police of a monthly bribe for protection to vice in
Chinatown clear to the fraudulent landing of ex
cluded Chinese by evasion of the Federal law. Now
a cry is raised that our latest exposure, consisting
merely of the publication of the official records, will
injure San Francisco by prejudicing the Chinese mer
chant class, whose presence is absolutely requisite in
the enlargement of our trade with their country. We
deny that exposure of violations of the law will have
any such effect. The system we expose injures the
merchant class, not its exposure. We insist that mer
chants and their bona fide wives and families shall
have the entry rights and freedom of transit and com
munication required by them, as by all merchants, in
the prosecution of their business. We affirm, aiso,
that they would have and enjoy these rights unob
structed and be treated with the same consideration
and courtesy accorded to their class of other races if
the law and treaty were uprightly administered.
As long as craft, cunning and corruption are in ac
tion to defeat the law by landing the excluded classes
the excepted classes and the merchants will suffer.
We believe that the Chinese merchants understand
this. We believe that they resent the landing of boat
women as merchants" wives. We are sure that Mr. Ho
Vow, the Consul General, appreciates the force of
what we say. He is a gentleman of extraordinary in
telligence, with talent^ and training that would make
him a credit to any race, and he ought to know that it
is to the best interests of his people that American
officials administer the exclusion law strictly and
justly. That is what The Call is trying to secure.
The place to begin is with the American officials, if
the law is evaded it is by reason of their corruption or
carelessness, their lack of honesty or their lack of in
telligence. What we are doing is not to the injury of
San Fr2ncisco, but for its benefit. If we succeed in
making this port noted for the best and strictest en
forcement of the exclusion law, we have made San
Francisco the best city in the United States for the
Chinese merchants, upon whom our trade with that
country depends.
We must be credited with discrimination in the mat
ter. When Consul General Ho Vow asked for greater
Harness and justice to the Chinese merchants he was
attacked by the Examiner and charged with trying to
punch a hole in the exclusion law. That paper and
others like it make a sensational play of the Chinese
question, and dump merchants and all together. It
hns never done any good by that policy. When the
party it represents was administering the law corrup
tion and bribery were so rank that buzzards fled to
their carrion for fresh air.
We are not making a sensation, but are deter
mined to stop the corruption or the stupidity, or
bcth. now plainly evident in the administration of the
law.
Give the excepted Chinese all their rights, without
bribe or price, but teach the excluded classes that
r.either by corruption nor craft can they get rights
that are denied by the law, is the policy we have in
view.

Rumpus on the Doric Just
Before the Mail Boat
Got Away.
Eleven Out of Twenty-Six Austrian
Contract Laborers Bound for !
Hawaii Made Good Their
Escape From Ship.
The Occidental and Oriental Steamship
Company's steamer Doric sailed for the
Orient via Honolulu yesterday with a very
large cargo and a crowd of cabin passen
gers. Among the latter were Chang Hai
Kwan of the Chinese embassy in Wash
ington, who is nolng home. Commanders
A. R. Condon nr.d George Cowle, U. S. N.,
were also pas3engers. The latter goes out
to take charge of the machine shops at
Cavlte, while the former will take com
mand of one of the warships. J. H. Clai
borne, secretary for Pope & Talbot, Is
making the trip for his health. He takes
with him some very fine samples of Cali
fornia woods and will exhibit them in
China and Japan.
There was a big rumpus just before the
Doric got away. Twenty-six contract la
borers were supposed to sail on the vessel,
but only fifteen of them materialized. The
men are Austrians and came to the United
States under' contract to work in the
Hawaiian canefields. When they reached
here they learned what they would have
to do In the islands, and all made a break
for liberty. Eleven of them are still at
largo, but when the Doric sailed the other
fifteen were 1 securely locked up in the
forepeak of the ship with a strong guard
over them. The Southern Pacific detec
tives are now on the trail of the escapes,
and if they are caught they will be sent
on to Honolulu on the next steamer.
Nippon Mara Arrives.
The Japanese mail steamer Nippon
Maru arrived from the Orient via Hono
lulu yesterday. There were no new cases
of plague reported on the islands, but,
nevertheless, Dr. Klnyoun ordered the
vessel into quarantine. Late in the after
noon the cabin passengers from Hong
kong and Yokohama were landed, but the
Honolulu passengers will be kept aboard
until the vessel is released. The cabin
passengers on the Nippon Maru were:
Mr. and Mrs. E. D. Sims and child. V. R.

The Nippon Maru brought over 480 Asi
atics In the steerage. Of these 266 are
Chinese and 214 Japanese. The latter will
all be landed, unless it can be proven they
come here as contract laborers. Among
the Chinese there is the usual percentage
of "native sons."

THE GOLDEN AGE OF ENGLISH LITERATURE
Copyright, 1900, by Seymour Eaton.
GOLDEIV /\GES OF" LITERATURE.
XVII. THE ELIZABETHAN AGE.
BY VIDA D. SCUDDER.
Golden is a good adjective to apply to
Elizabethan literature, for that literature
Is all aglow with light. The sunshine of
the great Elizabethan day is pure and
fresh and Joyous at the beginning, in the
poetry of Sidney and Spenser; it is glo
rious with radiant warmth in the magnifi
cent work of Shakespeare; it is increas
ingly sad, though lovely, and suffused
with tragic crimson at last, as it stoops to
evening in the Jacobean drama.
We must remember that the Elizabeth
an period lasted really only a very short
time. Centuries had dragged their slow
length along with only here and there a
voice lifted to break the stillness; then
FUddenly, in the last quarter of the six
teenth century, England burst forth in a
chorus of song. We call the literary pc-
ing a quarter of a century more. All the
forces, however, which created the litera
ture of the renaissance were well under
way before Shakespeare died; after him
they were in their decline, and no new
method or power manifested itself till the
dying renaissance, the dominant puritan
ism, and the promise of the stately classi
cal reaction, all met in the work of Mil
ton.
From 1573 to MM is less than half a cen
tury, but in s-pniflcanr-e and beauty it
means more than all the ages that had
gone before. And it witnessed a complete
and profoundly interesting literary devel
opment In three clearly marked phases.
The first phase lasted until the publica
tion of the first three books of the "Fae
rie Queen" in 1590. This is a .non-dra
matic period, though the drama is begin
ning. Sir Philip Sidney and Spenser are
its two characteristic men; the work of
Spenser in particular spans and sums it
all. The rest of the Elizabethan age 1 â¢
dominated by the drama, and with tho
drama it passes through two distinc*
moods, though we cannot put our finger
riod that followed the Elizabethan age,
but it dees not really coincide with the
reign of the great Queen. She had as
cended the throne tn 15ii7, and during the
first half of her reign a hush rested upon
the land. We cannot wonder at this when
we realize that men were yet breathless
as It were with the shock and terror of
the experiences inflicted on the nation in
the reign of Queen Mary. Men whose
fathers had perhaps burned at the stake
were in no mood to chant madrigals. But
in the silence of the first twenty years of
Elizabeth's reign the keen observer can
trace ample signs of promise. All things
Indeed made ready for a great imagina
tive expansion. The foundations of the
national power hud at last been laid firm.
The young nation was at peace within,
enjoying a new commercial prosperity;
abroad she was measuring herself in no
ble warfare against Spain, a heroic foe.
Excited by the still recent discovery of
new worlds and of the classic past, men
looked away from the heavens whereon
their gaze all through the middle ages
had been fixed, beheld with a thrill of
freedom the horizons of earth ever widen
ing receding, beckoning, and felt them
selves, with Puck, able to clap a girdle
round the earth. A new temper was aris
ing, a temper of unbounded enthusiasm
for human art, beauty and learning, for
human passion and for human life. Tread
softly, for we are entering the days of
Shakespeare, the days of Spenser also and
of Sir Philip Sidney, of Bacon and Hooker
and Raleigh and Ben Jnnson. the days of
the sweetest lyrics England has ever
heard, of a noble reflective prose, of a su
preme drama.
It was in the year 1579 that three nota
ble books were publishedâ "Euphues," a
quaint romance which attempted the un
accustomed feat of describing with what
tried to be realism the experiences of a
young gentleman ot the day; North's
translation of "Plutarch's Lives." a book
on which Shakespeare was nourished, and
Spenser's lovely series of pastoral ec
logues, the "Shepherd's Calendar." The
Elizabethan period of literature had be-
To mark its close Is lees easy. We may
take the death of the Queen. In 1603, the
year after that in which "Hamlet" was
acted. Or. and this is more sensible, we
may take the death of Shakespeare, in
1616 Or we may go on still further, for
great things continued to be written dur-
on the exact elate when one mood changed
Into the other.
Let us look at the first phase. When
first the mysterious Impulse toward ar
tistic expression befcan to stir, men did
not take It very seriously. They toyed
with l!f> and art, poetry and prose. They
were â¢"empasslonrd" to u?e a fine word
of Spenser's, with felicity of phrase.
They tried countless literary experiments.
What, for instance, was the right way of
writing Enpllah verse? Should they copy
the quantitative, unrhymetl measures of
the ancients? Yes. answered for a tlmo
some of the best critics. Absurd though
this answer appear to us, we cannot won
der that it was Riven then. For the diff
nity and beauty of classic meters fell
fresh on people's ears, and of English
models they had few or none. So they set
to work solemnly to concoct hexameters,
sajjphics, what you will â an extraordinary
work they made of it. But the lovely,
new-born verse of English song laughed
at their pedantry, and her laughter rip
pled through their veins like music, and
in spite of themselves they began to sing
âthese would-be learned poets. Soon they
became intoxicated with the beauty of
their own words. They did not approve of
rhyme, but rhyme they did. with delicate
ease and abundance. They wanted to
write solemn quantitative verse, and mel
odies infinite in variety and charm rose
unbidden to their lips. No lyrical devel
opment has ever been seen like that of the
Elizabethan age.
What caused the whole nation to break
forth at once into sinking? Who can tell?
The more we study the more amazed we
grow at the singing quality that was in
the Elizabethan air. inspiring not only
great poets like Sidney and Spenser, but
al?o hosts of minor singers, unnamed and
unknown. The numerous anthologies pub
lished durlnnr this period attest the
strength of the lyrical Impulse. Their very
names are redolent of fantasy and sweet
ness. "The Paradise of Dainty Devices."
"A Gorgeous Gallery of Gallant Inven
tions." "The Phoenix's Nest." "The Pas
sionate Pilgrim." "England's Helicon."
"Davison's Poetical Rhapsody" arjj some
of them. Several of these have been re
printed In attractive modern editions. To
turn their pages is to wander through a
fragrant garden, breathing spice and
sweetness on every side.
Note â This study will be concluded to
morrow.
WILLIAM SHAKESPEARE.

TO TEACH CITY
GOVERNMENT IN
PUBLIC SCHOOLS
Board of Education Has the
Proposition Now Under
Consideration.
Directors Mark and Kincaid Approve
the Practical Study and Will In
troduce It on a Small
Scale Next Term.
Â»
The Board of Education, is now consid
ering a plan to introduce the practical
study of municipal government in the
public schools ot this city. The plan con
templates the operation of a "school city,"
with ail the accompanying official depart
ments and presiding heads, and has been
successfully conducted in one of the
Fchools across the bay.
The fact that articles have been pub
lished recently In which the successful
operation of the plan in several of the
Mississippi Kiver States has been de
rcrlbed lias Inspired the local School
Board with the iuca of giving the Bubject
"The proposition," said Mrs. Kincald,
"iÂ« in its Ineipleney at the present time,
but we h<-i>e to make all the necessary
arrangements; to add this very Important
Ftudy of city jTovernment to the course
in one or Iwo of the largest schools."
Supfrlnter-derit of Schools Webster is
not so sanguine about the result. He is
rather opposed to the plan, but as he has
not examined thoroughly Into it he says
that he might be disposed to modify his
opinion soirewhal after further investi
erntion. Mr. Webster says that the danger
lifs in the probable introduction of a bad
cvjtcm of politics in the proposition and
a consequent lack of discipline, which
resulted when it was introduced In one of
the Ecgtern schools.
BUT ONE NEW
PLAGUE CASE
AT HONOLULU
Girl Thirteen Years of Age
Succumbs to the
Disease. \
President Dole Arouses a Storm of
Protest by Naming an Unpop
ular Fire Claims
Board.
' *
HONOLULU. April 10.â But one case of
plague has been reported since last ad
vices. The case was that of a half-caste
girl, aged 13 years, ending fatally. Two
suspicious cases of sickness were reported
on the 6th at Koolau, a small village on
this Island. The attending doctor reported
the cases as being plague. Two physicians
from this city were sent out, and, after
making an examination, declared that
there were no Indications of plague.
Hilo and Knhului are both clean again.
The Hilo people have declared their port
a clean one and request that no shipments
be made from Honolulu.
For the first time since the outbreak of
the plague the steamer Australia will be
allowed to carry steorage passengers from
this port for San Francisco. The vessel
will leave here on the 17th with her steer
age packed with men who have been wait-
Ing for months to get out of Honolulu.
By an executivo order bearing date of
the 2d inst. President Dole has created "a
court of commissioners to take evidence
of losses caused by the burning of China
town in Honolulu and to make awards
and judgments on such losses."
The court is created and commissioners
appointed by authority of President Me-
Ivinley. The members are: J. Alfred Ma
goon, George A. Davis, Lorin Andrews,
A. Noa Kepoikai and Alfred F. Judd Jr.
All are members of the bar. The an
nouncement of their appointment was not
received with satisfaction by the commu
nity; In fact it raised a wave of Indigna
tion. It is' considered proper that busi
ness men should be represented on the
board. The Chamber of Commerce has
1 protested against the court. The Japan
Â¦ ese and Chinese residents In mass-meet
ing have denounced it, and finally the
Council of State has refused to appro
priate the money necessary for the court
to carry on its work. The only way out
of the difficulty will be for President Dole
to request the resignations of the court
and then appoint new men who will be ac
ceptable to the business interests.
"I certainly will not ask the members of
the Court of Claims to resign," said Presi
dent Dole. But notwithstanding the Pres
ident's desire not to change the court, it
is believed that public opinion will compel
him to change his mind.

BUSY TIMES FOR THE
DOG SHOW OFFICIALS
Closing Pays in Which Entries May
Be Made Bring a Rush of Work
to Kennel Club Officers.
Probably the busiest place in the busy
city just now is the office of the San
Francisco Kennel Club. 238 Montgomery
street. With a larger clerical force than
ever before employed, and working from
8 a. m. until 10 p. m.. "Dave" Sinclair and
his assistants have all they can do to
cope with the rush of entries. Only six
days remain in which it Js possible to
enter a dog, and the owners of fine dogs
realize that it is imperative that they be
entered at once. No pedigree is necessary
to show a dog, for once before the Judge
the candidate for the blue ribbon is Judg
ed entirely on his own merits.
Among the notable cocker spaniels en
tered is a red puppy dog owned by Mrs.
B. Clark. Mrs. D. H. Hogan has entered
a field spaniel puppy dog that will make
his older competitors do their best to de
feat him. The bull terrier men are espe
cially energetic. John D. Spreckels has
entered his kennel and will try conclu
sions with all the cracks the other fan
ciers may import from \ England and thÂ«Â»
East.
Mrs. Timothy Hopkins will show her
mastiff, Lola Montez, by Chlno Don ex
Lola. This young mastiff has developed
wonderfully since last year, when she
easily won the first prize in the puppy
class. Mrs. James L. Flood will show a
magnificent brace of mastiffsâ Caesar * .
by Roderick Dhu ex Baby Ruth, and
Myra F. by Major II ex Queen Bess. Col
onel Flood will show his handsome collie
Maxwell.
Handsome prizes are offered by the club
for trick dogs Besides the regular money
prizes, trophies ranging in value from $25
to $75 are offered in nearly every class,
besides a large and varied assortment or
medals.

the Siberian cost, but this year not a
wind jammer will be chartered. Instead
tramp steamers will do the work, and the
Tyr is the first of the fleet to get here,
and will make her initial trip up the river
next Saturday.
Max Kalish. manager of the Humboldt
Steamship Company, ia on a flying visit
to the city. He wa.s in Eureka last week
and leaves for Seattle next Friday. The
steamer Humboldt is now on the run be
tween Seattle and Skaguay.
The naval reserve training ship Marion
will not come down from the navy yard
for three weeks. It will be a fortnight
before the Government can finish paint-
Ing- and cleaning her. The Sunday after
she is turned over Captain James and his
jolly tars will go to Mare Island and bring
her down to San Francisco.
The ex-transport Westminster has been
purchased by John RosenfelcTs Sons and
will in future carry coal from Nanaimo,
B. C, to San Francisco.

GETTING CLOSE UPON
CHARGES OF FORGERY
Photographs, Checks and Letters Take
Place of Sensational Testimony
in the Fair Case.
CONTRACT SIGNATURE BETWEEN TWO OTHERS TAKEN FROM CHECKS.
WITNESSES who saw and heard
have made their exit in the Fair
case for the time being and In
their place has come the expert
with his lines and dots and angles. The
curtain has fallen on the first act of the
respondent's play and the three charac
ters. Sylva, Dunand and Slmpton, have
come and gone. They were called to
prove that Senator Fair and Mrs. Craven
v.-ere not married with the full solemnity
of the law.
The second act will be all for the expert,
and his work will tend toward the proof
that the signature of the marriage con
tract is a forgery. And the pencil deeds
will be again introduced by the defense in
an attempt to show that Mrs. Craven's I
statement on cross-examination that the ]
Senator provided for her by a gift of the I
Mission and Sansome street properties
v;as false. It rr>ay come to pass, if the
opposing attorneys reach a unanimity,
that the evidence taken In the pencil-deed
case may be read into the record of the,
present case. Should this agreement be
consummated much time and expense will
be paved both parties.
When the session opened yesterday
morning Theodore Kytka. expert in chi- j
rographv and photographer of documents. â¢
was on "hand with a large box "as black
as starless night." This is Kytka's own j
and looks like the trunk mystery appar- i
atua of e-ome Mack magician. The Pences
and their client expected to see the ghost '
of Senator Fair drawn from {he recesses
of the cavernous sable box, but all that
was taken from its depths was a photo
graph or two, a negative and some speci
mens of handwriting.
Before Kytka mounted the witness
stand and told the court what ho knew
about documentary photography and what
an expert he was. Charles E. Stewart, the
accountant of the Fair estate, identified
the signature of James G. Fair on a batch
of checks. Attorney Crothers passed
check after check, signed by Senator Fair,
into the evidence. There were twenty-one
of them In the first bundle, all of them
made between the 20th and 2Rthof May,
1592, the time at which the alleged mar
riage contract is claimed to have been
executed and signed. Another bundle of
checks, made and signed In September,
1594. the time of the pencil deeds, was in
troduced and â¢ admitted, besides letters
written at the some time.
The Pence attorneys made no objection
to the admission of those checks and let
ters and the cross-examination of Stewart
was brief. Lafe Pence asked for other
checks slpned by Senator Fair and was
satisfied with the promise of attorneys to
produce them at the rail.
On a r*f|UO3t from T>afe Pence to post
pone the trial until to-day at 2 p. m. the
court adjourned the case. Mrs. Craven's
attorneys were desirous of examining a
laryre photograph of the admitted signa
tures of Fair studded around the contract
sfpnaturp. Professor Ames will be the
expert on handwriting called by the Fair
attorneys.

CHICAGO, April 17.â Wheat began the day
dull and easy. There was no encouragement
In cables. The rains continued to be regarded
as favorable to the crop and cash people re
ported nothing doing. The long stuff soon be
gan to ccme out more liberally, part of It be
cause it had to be sold, some in sympathy
with the slump In corn. The pit was gloomy
and well nigh deserted. Liquidation was not
in large lots, but the small lots came in plen
tifully, meeting Bmall demand. The excellent
report of the condition of the crop In Kansas
was probably a consideration with some, for
while there were differences of opinion as to
the size of the crop reaspning from the official
figures as to percentage and acreage it was
generally admitted that it would be liberal.
Bradstreet's figures showing a decrease In the
world's visible of 2,373,000 bushels caused a
momentary check in the decline â but only a
momentary one. May opened a shade lower at

The corn market was dull and weak. The

under yesterday. July closed lc down.
The oats market closed weak. There was
a continuance of May liquidation in oats which
has been under way for Beveral days. May
closed %c lower.
There was a big provision trade, the feature
of which was pork. Hogs were higher and re

general demand and shorts saw fit to cover.
Lard and ribs relinquished some of their early
gains, but closed steady, while pork, although
there was a dip from the early bulge, re
covered and closed strong. May pork closed

The War Tax.
The New York circular of Henry Clews *ays:
"Home conditions are favorable to continued
ease in money for some time to come. The
April Erttlements are oxer and all reports In
dicate an abundance of loanable funds at the
interior. The sui^ly of currency is consider
ably larger than a year ago and is being stead
â¢!y increased under the operations of the new
currency law. The chief danger to the money
roarkri. and one that should not be overlooked.
Is the ponsibility of Inconveniently large sums
bfing lacked up in the United States Treasury
through the operation of the Internal revenue
law imposed by the war with Spain. Secretary
Gaco has estimate a surplus ot $70.0n0.0iÂ» for
the fiscal year ending June Si. 1900. and $(.2.000 -
OOd Jor the fiscal year ending 1501. It is need
less to point out the Injury, not merely to Vv ail
rtreet. but to what if vastly more important.
the legitimate business interests of the coun
try if funds are allowed to congest in the
Treasury in this fashion. It might be jx>ssible
for the Secretary to d<*po*it a large portion
of Â«hese accumulations in the depositary bajiks.
but' considering the present state of public
prejudice this method would doubtless expose
the administration to the silly charge of favor
itism to the banks, and raise an issue that is
t>est avoided. There is but one true course,
and that Is for Cor.sress at this session to re
ntal the superfluous and mast Irritating fea
tures of the Internal revenue law. co as to keep
a closer balance between receipts and expendi
tures A large surplus may be considered a
Â«i*:n of good management in private business.
t>ut in labile business It is bad management
and dangerous temptation to all sorts of ex
tiavacani and vicious legislation. Our Con-
Kressmen should be obliged to reduce the reve
nue before the present session clones, otnerwis
they must accept the responsibility for bring
lnr the present tide of business activity to an
untimely halt."

Available Grain Supply.
NEW YORK. April 17.â Special cable and tel
egraphic communications to Bradstreet's show
the following changes In available supplies
from last account:
Wheat, United States and Canada, east of
the Rocky Mountains, decrease, 1,273,000
bushels.
Liverpool Corn Trade News, afloat- for and
in Europe, decrease. 1,100,000 bushels. Total
supply, decrease, 2,373,000 bushels.
Corn, United States and Canada, east of the
Rocky Mountains. Increase, 165.000 bushels.
Oats, United States and Canada, east of the
Rocky Mountains, increase, 119,000 bushels.
Among the more important decreases re
ported to Bradstreet's not given in the official
visible supply. are those of 300,000 bushels at
Northwestern interior elevators, 240,000 bushels
at Manitoba storage points, 159.000 bushels at
Depot Harbor, 74.000 bushels at Portland, Me.,
and 71,000 bushels at East Dubuque.
The principal decreases are those of 148.000
bushels at Port Huron, 132,000 bushels at Mil
waukee private elevators and 52,000 bushels
at Chicago private elevators.
The aggregate stock of wheat held at Port
land, Or., and Tacoma and Seattle, Wash., de
creased 7000 bushels last week.

London Market.
NEW YORK. April 17.â The Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
Business was only half resumed after the holi
days in the markets here to-day. The tone
was 3teady. For the war loan special settle
ment the contango began at 5% and finished at
5. not co stiff as was exDected.
Americans opened a shade above parity, but
arbitraeeura were all addressing the list. New
Tork cam? very dissappointing, causing the
worst prices. They suddenly improved toward
the close on heavy buying of Norfolk and
Western, said to be for Kuhn, Loeb & Co. The
stock continued to mount in the street and
others sympathized. A general rally is now ex
pected on New York's Initiative.
Tlntcs spurted to 60%. a fresh record. Parts
has the market well In hand and Is squeezing
the Ware, who are plentiful in England and
still more in Scotland. Anacondas were heavy,
Americans selling them 94 down to 10%. The
copper fortnightly statistics show stocks in
creased 12^5 tons and supplies increased 2010.
Money was in demand and there was a mod
erate Funily. Half the amount due the bank
was repaid, the rest being received at 5 per
cent. The bank did a good business In dis
counts ct 4 uer cent. Of gold Â£12,000 came in

NOTEâ The high and low waters occur at
thÂ« city front (Mission-street wharf) about

the- height of tide Is the same at both places.
NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of thÂ»
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column rives the second tide of
the day. the third time column the third tld>
and the' last or rieht hand column gives th*
last tide of the day. except when there are buf.
three tides, as sometimes occurs. The heigh:*
given are in addition to the soundings on tha
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â > precedes the height.
and then th* number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference I<> the mean of the lower low watc-*.

CHICAGO, April 17.â Wheat began the day
dull and easy. There was no encouragement
In cables. The rains continued to be regarded
as favorable to the crop and cash people re
ported nothing doing. The long stuff soon be
gan to ccme out more liberally, part of It be
cause it had to be sold, some in sympathy
with the slump In corn. The pit was gloomy
and well nigh deserted. Liquidation was not
in large lots, but the small lots came in plen
tifully, meeting Bmall demand. The excellent
report of the condition of the crop In Kansas
was probably a consideration with some, for
while there were differences of opinion as to
the size of the crop reaspning from the official
figures as to percentage and acreage it was
generally admitted that it would be liberal.
Bradstreet's figures showing a decrease In the
world's visible of 2,373,000 bushels caused a
momentary check in the decline â but only a
momentary one. May opened a shade lower at
wiVuCti-V, and sold down to 66VÂ»c, closing at
that figure, %c under yesterday. July opened
a shade down at 68c to 67% c, sold down to
67%i(C7^c and closed He depressed at 67Vic.
The corn market was dull and weak. The
demand was In every way slack and liquida
tion caused a decline. May closed weak, lc
under yesterday. July closed lc down.
The oats market closed weak. There was
a continuance of May liquidation in oats which
has been under way for Beveral days. May
closed %c lower.
There was a big provision trade, the feature
of which was pork. Hogs were higher and re
ceipts only 14,000 head. . There was a good
general demand and shorts saw fit to cover.
Lard and ribs relinquished some of their early
gains, but closed steady, while pork, although
there was a dip from the early bulge, re
covered and closed strong. May pork closed
22V4C over yesterday, July 17Vjc up; May lard
2Vic improved. July 2Vic better and May ribs
closed unchanged, July 2%c lower.

The War Tax.
The New York circular of Henry Clews *ays:
"Home conditions are favorable to continued
ease in money for some time to come. The
April Erttlements are oxer and all reports In
dicate an abundance of loanable funds at the
interior. The sui^ly of currency is consider
ably larger than a year ago and is being stead
â¢!y increased under the operations of the new
currency law. The chief danger to the money
roarkri. and one that should not be overlooked.
Is the ponsibility of Inconveniently large sums
bfing lacked up in the United States Treasury
through the operation of the Internal revenue
law imposed by the war with Spain. Secretary
Gaco has estimate a surplus ot $70.0n0.0iÂ» for
the fiscal year ending June Si. 1900. and $(.2.000 -
OOd Jor the fiscal year ending 1501. It is need
less to point out the Injury, not merely to Vv ail
rtreet. but to what if vastly more important.
the legitimate business interests of the coun
try if funds are allowed to congest in the
Treasury in this fashion. It might be jx>ssible
for the Secretary to d<*po*it a large portion
of Â«hese accumulations in the depositary bajiks.
but' considering the present state of public
prejudice this method would doubtless expose
the administration to the silly charge of favor
itism to the banks, and raise an issue that is
t>est avoided. There is but one true course,
and that Is for Cor.sress at this session to re
ntal the superfluous and mast Irritating fea
tures of the Internal revenue law. co as to keep
a closer balance between receipts and expendi
tures A large surplus may be considered a
Â«i*:n of good management in private business.
t>ut in labile business It is bad management
and dangerous temptation to all sorts of ex
tiavacani and vicious legislation. Our Con-
Kressmen should be obliged to reduce the reve
nue before the present session clones, otnerwis
they must accept the responsibility for bring
lnr the present tide of business activity to an
untimely halt."

Available Grain Supply.
NEW YORK. April 17.â Special cable and tel
egraphic communications to Bradstreet's show
the following changes In available supplies
from last account:
Wheat, United States and Canada, east of
the Rocky Mountains, decrease, 1,273,000
bushels.
Liverpool Corn Trade News, afloat- for and
in Europe, decrease. 1,100,000 bushels. Total
supply, decrease, 2,373,000 bushels.
Corn, United States and Canada, east of the
Rocky Mountains. Increase, 165.000 bushels.
Oats, United States and Canada, east of the
Rocky Mountains, increase, 119,000 bushels.
Among the more important decreases re
ported to Bradstreet's not given in the official
visible supply. are those of 300,000 bushels at
Northwestern interior elevators, 240,000 bushels
at Manitoba storage points, 159.000 bushels at
Depot Harbor, 74.000 bushels at Portland, Me.,
and 71,000 bushels at East Dubuque.
The principal decreases are those of 148.000
bushels at Port Huron, 132,000 bushels at Mil
waukee private elevators and 52,000 bushels
at Chicago private elevators.
The aggregate stock of wheat held at Port
land, Or., and Tacoma and Seattle, Wash., de
creased 7000 bushels last week.

Business was only half resumed after the holi
days in the markets here to-day. The tone
was 3teady. For the war loan special settle
ment the contango began at 5% and finished at

stock continued to mount in the street and
others sympathized. A general rally is now ex
pected on New York's Initiative.
Tlntcs spurted to 60%. a fresh record. Parts
has the market well In hand and Is squeezing
the Ware, who are plentiful in England and
still more in Scotland. Anacondas were heavy,
Americans selling them 94 down to 10%. The
copper fortnightly statistics show stocks in
creased 12^5 tons and supplies increased 2010.
Money was in demand and there was a mod
erate Funily. Half the amount due the bank
was repaid, the rest being received at 5 per

counts ct 4 uer cent. Of gold Â£12,000 came in

the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of thÂ»
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column rives the second tide of
the day. the third time column the third tld>
and the' last or rieht hand column gives th*
last tide of the day. except when there are buf.
three tides, as sometimes occurs. The heigh:*
given are in addition to the soundings on tha
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â > precedes the height.
and then th* number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference I<> the mean of the lower low watc-*.

New Evidence of the Effect.
A South Dakota man, W. A. Dawley,
Sturgis, explains the condition si me
men get into from coffee drinking. Let
it be understood that coffee seems to
agree with some people well enough,
but \\ la p a person finds it does not
agree it is better to leave it off. He
says: '"I used coffee about twenty
years, and quit it September 30, 1898.
1 had become almost a complete wreck,
thin in rich and emaciated, dyspepsia
and indigestion of the worst orden
nerves shattered, no appetite, slept very
little, and my blood was so thin that the
slightest breeze chilled me through.
'"Medicines gave no relief. I had
about given up when I finally concluded
to investigate and see if it was really
true, as I had read in a number of your
statements, that coffee acted like a poi-
son to some people. It was an easy
matter to leave off coffee when I took
Postum Food Coffee, for the food cof-
fee taste! as good as the other, and, to
my' surprise, it set good on my weak
stomach.
"I have been buying the Postum
through my grocer." Mr. James Meyer,
and always have followed directions
carefully. I Irked it from the first and
have drank Postum three times a day
ever fince. Have pained twenty pounds
in weight, my appetite is better than
ever before, my sleep perfect, dyspepsia
is a thins; of the past, and my nerves are
what they should be. I can vouch for
the statement that Postum Food Coffee
'makes red blood' and plenty of it. My
son, Amos Dawley of this city, has been
completely cured of dyspepsia in three
months by quinine coffee and using
Postum Food Coffee This is given
you as an honest testimonial of the
priceless value of your (tS* me) life-
saving food drink. You are welcome to
use my name if you want" Respect-
fully. ' ':â¢'Â¦ <;/^jL

Internal Revenue Appointments.
Levi S. Bixby.of Oakland has been re
instated as storekeeper gauger by order
of the Secretary of the Treasury. Mr.
Bixby was a storekeeper ln the revenue
service from IS9I to 1833, and Is reinstated
under the provision of the civil service
law extending the privilege to veterans
of the Civil War. He served in the Mas
sachusetts troops from ISG2-65.
Wlnfleld Scott of Kern, Cal., has been
appointed Deputy Collector, and Assigned
to office duty ln San Francisco. Mr. Scott
was formerly Assessor of Kern County.
DR. STIMPSON'S SON
HURT WHILE WHEELING
OAKLAND. April 17.-While bicycling
on San Pablo avenue, near Seventeenth
street, this afternoon Earl Stlmpson, 13
years old. son of O. W. Stimpson, was
run down by a team, or another wheel
man, and so badly injured that he Is In
grave danger of death. The boy's skull
was fractured and he sustained a severe
concussion of the brain. The lad man
aged to reach his home ln a half con
scious condition. He was unable to say
more than that he had been in a collision.
He resides at 698 Twenty-fourth street,
only a few blocks from the scene of tho
accident. The parents called Dr. Robert
T. Stratton, who performed an operation
on the skull, but pronounced the Injury
to be very serious.
This evening the police were notified of
the accident and detectives were detailed
to search for the now unknown person
who ran Into the boy. The accident oc
curred at the crossing of San Pablo ave
nue and Seventeenth street, and it Ist sup
posed the collision was caused by some
team or wheel that was running across
young Stlmpson's path. The boy's father
is a well-known veterinary surgeon.

Session of the Grand Chapter â Offi
cers Elected for Ensuing Term.
There were present about two hundred
and fifty representatives yesterday m6rn
lng at the opening of the Grand Chapter
of the Royal Arch Masons at the annual

port of the officers showed a very satis
factory state of afTairs during the paat
year The afternoon was taken up in the

Special Dlppatch to The Call
HOLLISTER, April 17.â At noon yester

years'* poured the contents of a kerosene

Marsden Manson Lectures on Russia.
An interesting lecture on Russia" was
delivered last nirht by Mnrsden Manson,
Commissioner of Public Works, at the
Young Women's Christian Association.
1259 \. i arrell street. The lecture was il
lustrated with a series of stereopticon
views which had been taken by Mr. Man
son during his attendance at a recent in
ternational congress of geologists In St.
Petersburg. He spoke entertainingly of
his travels and showed among others the
following pictures: The rivers Neva and
Volga, the naval station at Kronstadt,
Academy of Fine Arts in St. Petersburg,
the steppes of Russia, Great Trans-Sibe
rian Railway and Moscow. Before the
lecture a piano duet was rendered by Miss
A. Grass and Miss M. Webb and a man
dolin solo by Miss Lois Wheaton.
ATHLETIC EVENTS AT
MAY DAY BENEFIT
Arrangements for the May day benefit
for the San Francisco Public School
Teachers' Annuity Society, to be held at
Glen Park, are rapidly approaching com
gletion, and an enjoyable day is promised
y the promoters of the project. A num
ber of athletic events have been arranged
to take place in the afternoon. Only two
pupils from each school may enter the
races, with the exception of the 100-yard
hill- climbing race for boys of the eighth
and ninth grades, when four pupils may
contest.
The colors adopted for the day are those
of the two universities, blue, gold and red.
For the sake of harmony all badges and
exterior decorations of booths will have
those colors. Headquarters will be es
tablished near the theater.
Committees and schools are working
zealously in the cause, and the member
ship has passed the 500 mark. Schools not
managing booths can assist greatly by so
liciting supplies, orders for which can be
forwarded to T. H. McCarthy, care of Su
perintendent of School's office, City Hall.
Teachers must collect cash and unsold
tickets not later than Friday. April 27,
and no commissions are to be paid to pu
pils for selling tickets after that date.

Martin GilleRt Expires.
Martin Gillett, one of the earliest set
tlers in this State and once a prominent
business man, expired yesterday morning.
He was 82 years old and death was the
result of old age. Gillett was well known
in this city, and during the past few
years of his life was particularly popular
along the water front.
SUDDEN DEATH OF
A RECEIVING TELLER
William Cunningham of the Bank of
California Passes Away After
a Brief Illness.
William Cunningham, receiving teller of
the Bank of California and one of the
best-known yachtsmen in the State,
passed away yesterday after a short Ill
ness. His taking off has been a severe
blow to his relatives and friends. He was
B son of the late Commodore R. B. Cun
ningham, U. S. N.'v and brother of Mrs.
M. E. Steele and Mrs. J. W. C. Maxwell.
Deceased came to California in ISSS, and
during his residence In this State was con
nected at different times with Thomas H,
Belby, Wells. Fargo & Co.. the San Fran
cisco Bank. Haywards Bank, Safe De
posit Company, and at the time of his
death was with the Bank of California.
He was a member of the leading yacht
clubs and was considered an expert Bailor.
He was a native of Washington, D. C.

DESCENDANT OF KING
LILOA PASSES AWAY
Death of Robert Hoapili Baker, a
Warm Friend of the Late
Kalakaua.
HONOLULU, April 10.â Robert Hoapill
Kekaipukaala Baker of the royal family
of Llloa of Hawaii died on the 4th Inst.
and was burled yesterday.'
Robert Hoapill Baker, or, as he was
best known in his lifetime, Hoapili
Baker, was a descendant in the sixth de
gree of Liloa, King of Hawaii. His great
grandfather was Napuupahoehoe, high
chief of Maul, at about the time of the
discovery of the Islands by Captain Cook.
His father was Kekeleelku of Maul, and
Hoapill was born at Waikapu, Maui, in
IMS. He was educated at Lahainaluna.
Early in life he developed strong military
tastes and he was for many years an of
ficer in the household troops, for a time
a captain and later a colonel. He was a
warm personal friend of King Kalakaua,
and the King made him Governor of Maui,
a position he held for many years. In
the later years of his life he was not
actively engaged in business. He owned
considerable property and lived a retired
life of leisure.
Hoapili Baker accompanied Kalakaua
to San Francisco in 1890, and was with
him when he died, accompanying the re
mains back to Honolulu.
THREE FIGHTS SCHEDULED.
Fitzsimmons and Dunkhorst to Com
bat on April 30.
NEW YORK, April 17.â The decision of
the Police Commissioners to stop prize
fighting after May 1 probably will have
the effeqt of crowding all fights into the
next dozen days.
McGovern and Tommy Warren, the for
mer featherweight champion, who has
been in retirement for several years, will
fight twenty-five rounds at the Broadway
Athletic Club on Friday night.
Bob Fitzsimmons, Ed Dunkhorst and
the Hercules Athletic Club will post $500
each this week to guarantee the match
between the first-mentioned two, which
will be decided at the Hercules Club
house April 30.
Dal Hawkins, the California light
weight, and Jack Daly of Wilmington,
Del., will fight at the Hercules Athletic
Club next Monday night.

SANTA CRUZ, April 17.â James Watson,

GRAND LODGE KNIGHTS
AND LADIES OF HONOR
Showing Made by Reports of Grand
Officers â Rulers for Next Year
to Be Elected To-Day.
The first session of the Grand Lodge of
the Knights and Ladles of Honor of the
jurisdiction of California was held yester
day in Pythian Castle. Grand Protector
Z. T. Whitten presided and there were
present all the grand officers and dele
gates from the several lodges.
The sessions ln the forenoon and after
noon were taken up ln reading and con
sidering the reports of the grand officers.
That of the grand protector showed that
notwithstanding the increase of assess
ments at the session of the Supreme
Lodge last October the order is in better
condition than at any previous period in
its history. During the year Equity-
Lodge of Sacramento consolidated, with
Harmony Lodge of that city. A Â¦ new
lodge is being organized at Watsonville.
The report of the grand secretary show
ed that there had been seventeen deaths,
the beneficiaries receiving $30,000. There
are now 7&9 members in good standing, a
loss of seventeen by death and of fifty
seven who withdrew on account of the
increased rates of assessment. The Grand
Lodge receved $1385 during the year and
disbursed $919.
The first order of business this morn
ing will be the election of officers. New
opposition "has developed against the
grand protector and A. H. Anderson,, the
vice protector, who wants to advance to
first place. These are Dr. C. C. O'Donnell,
James P. Dockery and J. A. Colquhoun.
For grand secretary Mrs. Antoinette Graff
will oe a candidate against H. W. Qult
zow, ,the incumbent.
WILLING TO SELL
RECREATION PARK
At the regular meeting of the directors
of the Mechanics' Institute last nlyht the
recommendation of Messrs. P. J. Healy,
G. H. Wallis and R. J. Tausslg that the
Folsom-street property be sold was adopt
ed by the board. Real estate men compe
tent to judge appraise the property, now
known as Recreation Park, at $125,000, and
the board is willing to let It go at that fig
ure. It was stated last night that the
property cost 197 ,000 a few years ago but
has since cost the institute nearly $100,000
more. It brings in practically no income.
The institute, on the other hand. Is pay
ing interest on a mortgage of $400,000 on
the pavilion, and wants to be rid of some
of Its burdens.
It was decided to replace the office of
the librarian on the main floor where it
was formerly. Hereafter all books of ref
erence are to be plainly marked "Refer
ence" on the back, po as to avoid confu
sion. The directors are making an effort
to restore the library business to its for
mer basis.

SHIRT ESTABLISHED
SUICIDE'S IDENTITY
Dead Man Discovered to Be Frank
Merwin by the Man Who Gave
Him Article of Apparel.
The mystery which surrounded the man
who was found dead in the lodging house
at 317 Third street last Thursday has
been cleared Up. He was yesterday posi
tively identified as Frank Merwln and the
police and Morgue officials are satisfied.
Detectives AVrenn and Dinan, who were
detailed on the case, succeeded in tracing
his identity by means of the shirt, upon
which deceased had written a facetious
note shortly before he ended his life. Hart
Bouton, superintendent of the La Grand
and White laundries, settled all doubts
yesterday by stating that he knew de
ceased well and had given him the shirt
marked "1001." which was on the body.
Â¦ According to his statement Merwin was
a cook and had signed to act in that ca
pacity on the bark Felton, which was to
sail for Seattle and Nome the day follow
ing his tragic death.
Morgue Surgeon Leland reported to Cor
oner Cole that he had been unable to find
any lesion of the organs that would ac
count for the death of the man. He said
he found some rigidity of the heart, as
though there might have been paralysis of
that organ, but he did not think that the
indications were sufficient to be taken as
the cause of death. City Chemist Charles
L. Morgan, who analyzed the stomach, re
ported that he had failed to find any
traces of poison. He was of the opinion
that if the suicide had taken phosphorus
and strychnine pills traces of the poison
would be found.
FITZGERALD IS ON
TRIAL FOR HIS LIFE
Damaging Testimony Is Given by
George O'Malley, Who Was
Employed by Lennon.
Frwcial DisiiuUc-h to The Call.
REDWOOD CITY. April 17.â A jury was
secured this morning in the Fitzgerald
murder case and the taking of testimony
commenced this afternoon. The first wit
ness called for the prosecution was Coun
ty Surveyor W. B. Gilbert, who identified
and explained a plan of the dwelling
where the homicide occurred.
Sheriff J. H. Mansfield testified as to
certain photographs taken at the same
place about a week after the shooting.
Dr. J. L. Ross, who held an autopsy
upon the body of John Lennon on last
Christmas day. testified as to the cause
of death. Apart from the wound that
caused death he found Lennon to be a
strong, muscular and healthy man about
Fix feet tall and weighing about ISO
pounds. He was not allowed to give his
opinion as to the position of the body
when the wound was received.
George O'Malley was called and testified
that on December 24 last and prior thereto
he had been driving a team of Lennon'a
and living at the latter's house; that Fitz
gerald had also been living there for
some time; that witness arrived at the
place on Christmas eve about 7:30 o'clock,
having returned from a visit to Colma.
and found Lennon lying dead on the floor.
In the dining-room behind the door on the
floor he found an old Springfield rifle
which he identified as the property of
Fitzgerald. He also testified that early in
the morning of the 24th of December he
drove with Fitzgerald from Lennon's
place to Colma to catch the train for San
Francisco. On this trip Fitzgerald said
to witness: "I could put a bullet through
that (referring to Lennon) and noth
ing would be done to me." The examina
tion of the witness was incomplete when
court adjourned and will be resumed to
morrow morning.

THROWN FROM A BUGGY
AND LOSES HER LIFE
Fatal Accident Occurs to the Wife of
Deputy Assessor Irish of
Placerville.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PLACERVILLE, April 17.â Mrs. C. F.
Irish, wife of Deputy Assessor C F. Irish,
lost her life as the result of an accident
which occurred near Georgetown Sunday
night. Mr. and Mrs. Irish were driving to
their home, and while crossing the bridge
about a half-mile from the house their
horse, which was young and skittish, sud
denly swerved to one side, going off the
bridge. Mr. and Mrs. Irish were both
thrown from the buggy and Mrs. Irish
was thrown head first into a pile of rocks,
being rendered unconscious. Mr. Irish
was badly cut and bruised about the
head and sustained a deep scalp wound,
but he did not itrike hard enough to ren
der him unconscious.
When Mrs. Irish was examined It was
found that her left arm was bruised and
broken In two different places, one side of
her skull was laid bare from a wide gash
and she was completely paralyzed. She
never regained consciousness and died at
9 o'clock this morning.
Mrs. Irish was. prior to her marriage,
one of the best known and most successful
teachers ln the public schools of this
county.
WORK OF SAFE CRACKERS.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MODESTO, April 17.â The fire-proof safe
in the saloon of Henry Vogelman, on
Front street, was blown open some time
Sunday night and about $240 stolen.
The safe-cracker gained entrance- Into
the building through the ventilator in the
cellar. Access into the saloon from the
cellar was easy, as the door opens upward
and was not fastened. A hole three
eights of an inch ln size was bored
through the safe door near the lock, and
powder Inserted and exploded, which blew
the door open. Some of the tools used
were taken from a blacksmith shop ln
town and were left behind. The burglar
also took the content", of the cash regis
ter, securing about $8 or $9. The officers
are working on a clow but no arrests
have been made at this hour.

A TERRIBLE Instance of Chinese
brutality and lawlessness was re
lated to the police in Chinatown
for Investigation last night. Ac
cording to the verified report made to the
officers, the Mongolians have been taking
the law Into their own hands and punish
ing offenders by brutal methods, extend
ing even to attempted murder. Two Ce
lestials who were paid advance money to
join the exodus of fishermen to Alaska
and who squandered the coin over the
fan tan table and then hid until after the
steamers had sailed were captured by
their countrymen and, it is claimed, con
fined in an improvised prison, brutally
beaten and their throats slashed with
razors.

keep their engagements to their employ
ers, have had rewards placed on their

been posted in the principal thoroughfares
of the Chinese district. â¢

SEXTON ROSENER IS
SUMMONED BY DEATH
SAMUEL. ROSENER, for more than
ten years sexton of Temple Emanu-
El, dfed suddenly yesterday at his
home, 1611 Bush street. He was ill
less than half an hour. When he started
downtown early In the morning he was
apparently in perfect health. : He met
some friends about noontime and com
plained that he was suffering from a se
vere pain in his side, which had come on
rather suddenly and which was giving
him much uneasiness.
He was advised to hasten home and
take a rest. He arrived at his residence
a little after noon and a physician was
summoned. Before the doctors arrived
Rosenor applied a mustard plaster over
the region of his heart, where the pain
seemed most intense.
Complaining to his wife that the plaster
was too strong, he groaned and expired.
Deceased was a native of Germany, 61
years of age. He leaves a widow, four
grown daughters and two grown sons.
He was of a cheery disposition, popu
lar with all who knew him and was held
in high esteem by the congregation of
Temple Emanu-El.

John F. Ward Announces
His Marriage After
Ten Months.
Bride Is Miss Eva Finn, Daughter of
a Wealthy Easterner Who Re
cently Made California
His Home.
ALAMEDA, April 17.â Not until bride
and groom called upon her on Sunday last
to pay their respects did Mrs. Colonel J.
J. Tobin know that her brother. John F.
Ward, had become a benedict. Neither
did any of the many friends of Mr. Ward
flream that he had forsaken single bless
edness for matrimony. Mrs. Colonel
Tobin was taken quite aback herself by
the tardy announcement, and before her
recovery from the shock had confided the
interesting: information to a select few.
That is how the news got out.
To a bachelor's life Mr. Ward's friends
thought he was unalterably wedded.
Nearly 40 when he was a resident of Ala
meda they thought he had passed the
marrying age. No wonder, then, there
will be surprise when for the first time
public announcement is made that in June
last, in San Francisco, Mr. Ward married
Miss Eva Finn, daughter of an Eastern
capitalist who recently came to California
with his family to reside. Mrs. Ward is
handsome and accomplished.
Formerly Mr. Ward was enpraged ln the
real estate business In Alameda and later
was cashier of the now retired Encinal
Bank. At present he is identified with the
Hibernia Bank.

SAMUEL ROSENER.COFFEE ARGUMENT.
New Evidence of the Effect.
A South Dakota man, W. A. Dawley,
Sturgis, explains the condition si me
men get into from coffee drinking. Let
it be understood that coffee seems to
agree with some people well enough,
but \\ la p a person finds it does not
agree it is better to leave it off. He
says: '"I used coffee about twenty
years, and quit it September 30, 1898.
1 had become almost a complete wreck,
thin in rich and emaciated, dyspepsia
and indigestion of the worst orden
nerves shattered, no appetite, slept very
little, and my blood was so thin that the
slightest breeze chilled me through.
'"Medicines gave no relief. I had
about given up when I finally concluded
to investigate and see if it was really
true, as I had read in a number of your
statements, that coffee acted like a poi-
son to some people. It was an easy
matter to leave off coffee when I took
Postum Food Coffee, for the food cof-
fee taste! as good as the other, and, to
my' surprise, it set good on my weak
stomach.
"I have been buying the Postum
through my grocer." Mr. James Meyer,
and always have followed directions
carefully. I Irked it from the first and
have drank Postum three times a day
ever fince. Have pained twenty pounds
in weight, my appetite is better than
ever before, my sleep perfect, dyspepsia
is a thins; of the past, and my nerves are
what they should be. I can vouch for
the statement that Postum Food Coffee
'makes red blood' and plenty of it. My
son, Amos Dawley of this city, has been
completely cured of dyspepsia in three
months by quinine coffee and using
Postum Food Coffee This is given
you as an honest testimonial of the
priceless value of your (tS* me) life-
saving food drink. You are welcome to
use my name if you want" Respect-
fully. ' ':â¢'Â¦ <;/^jL

DR. STIMPSON'S SON
HURT WHILE WHEELING
OAKLAND. April 17.-While bicycling
on San Pablo avenue, near Seventeenth
street, this afternoon Earl Stlmpson, 13
years old. son of O. W. Stimpson, was
run down by a team, or another wheel
man, and so badly injured that he Is In
grave danger of death. The boy's skull
was fractured and he sustained a severe
concussion of the brain. The lad man
aged to reach his home ln a half con
scious condition. He was unable to say
more than that he had been in a collision.
He resides at 698 Twenty-fourth street,
only a few blocks from the scene of tho
accident. The parents called Dr. Robert
T. Stratton, who performed an operation
on the skull, but pronounced the Injury
to be very serious.
This evening the police were notified of
the accident and detectives were detailed
to search for the now unknown person
who ran Into the boy. The accident oc
curred at the crossing of San Pablo ave
nue and Seventeenth street, and it Ist sup
posed the collision was caused by some
team or wheel that was running across
young Stlmpson's path. The boy's father
is a well-known veterinary surgeon.

Marsden Manson Lectures on Russia.
An interesting lecture on Russia" was
delivered last nirht by Mnrsden Manson,
Commissioner of Public Works, at the
Young Women's Christian Association.
1259 \. i arrell street. The lecture was il
lustrated with a series of stereopticon
views which had been taken by Mr. Man
son during his attendance at a recent in
ternational congress of geologists In St.
Petersburg. He spoke entertainingly of
his travels and showed among others the
following pictures: The rivers Neva and
Volga, the naval station at Kronstadt,
Academy of Fine Arts in St. Petersburg,
the steppes of Russia, Great Trans-Sibe
rian Railway and Moscow. Before the
lecture a piano duet was rendered by Miss
A. Grass and Miss M. Webb and a man
dolin solo by Miss Lois Wheaton.
ATHLETIC EVENTS AT
MAY DAY BENEFIT
Arrangements for the May day benefit
for the San Francisco Public School
Teachers' Annuity Society, to be held at
Glen Park, are rapidly approaching com
gletion, and an enjoyable day is promised
y the promoters of the project. A num
ber of athletic events have been arranged
to take place in the afternoon. Only two
pupils from each school may enter the
races, with the exception of the 100-yard
hill- climbing race for boys of the eighth
and ninth grades, when four pupils may
contest.
The colors adopted for the day are those
of the two universities, blue, gold and red.
For the sake of harmony all badges and
exterior decorations of booths will have
those colors. Headquarters will be es
tablished near the theater.
Committees and schools are working
zealously in the cause, and the member
ship has passed the 500 mark. Schools not
managing booths can assist greatly by so
liciting supplies, orders for which can be
forwarded to T. H. McCarthy, care of Su
perintendent of School's office, City Hall.
Teachers must collect cash and unsold
tickets not later than Friday. April 27,
and no commissions are to be paid to pu
pils for selling tickets after that date.

Martin GilleRt Expires.
Martin Gillett, one of the earliest set
tlers in this State and once a prominent
business man, expired yesterday morning.
He was 82 years old and death was the
result of old age. Gillett was well known
in this city, and during the past few
years of his life was particularly popular
along the water front.
SUDDEN DEATH OF
A RECEIVING TELLER
William Cunningham of the Bank of
California Passes Away After
a Brief Illness.
William Cunningham, receiving teller of
the Bank of California and one of the
best-known yachtsmen in the State,
passed away yesterday after a short Ill
ness. His taking off has been a severe
blow to his relatives and friends. He was
B son of the late Commodore R. B. Cun
ningham, U. S. N.'v and brother of Mrs.
M. E. Steele and Mrs. J. W. C. Maxwell.
Deceased came to California in ISSS, and
during his residence In this State was con
nected at different times with Thomas H,
Belby, Wells. Fargo & Co.. the San Fran
cisco Bank. Haywards Bank, Safe De
posit Company, and at the time of his
death was with the Bank of California.
He was a member of the leading yacht
clubs and was considered an expert Bailor.
He was a native of Washington, D. C.

DESCENDANT OF KING
LILOA PASSES AWAY
Death of Robert Hoapili Baker, a
Warm Friend of the Late
Kalakaua.
HONOLULU, April 10.â Robert Hoapill
Kekaipukaala Baker of the royal family
of Llloa of Hawaii died on the 4th Inst.
and was burled yesterday.'
Robert Hoapill Baker, or, as he was
best known in his lifetime, Hoapili
Baker, was a descendant in the sixth de
gree of Liloa, King of Hawaii. His great
grandfather was Napuupahoehoe, high
chief of Maul, at about the time of the
discovery of the Islands by Captain Cook.
His father was Kekeleelku of Maul, and
Hoapill was born at Waikapu, Maui, in
IMS. He was educated at Lahainaluna.
Early in life he developed strong military
tastes and he was for many years an of
ficer in the household troops, for a time
a captain and later a colonel. He was a
warm personal friend of King Kalakaua,
and the King made him Governor of Maui,
a position he held for many years. In
the later years of his life he was not
actively engaged in business. He owned
considerable property and lived a retired
life of leisure.
Hoapili Baker accompanied Kalakaua
to San Francisco in 1890, and was with
him when he died, accompanying the re
mains back to Honolulu.
THREE FIGHTS SCHEDULED.
Fitzsimmons and Dunkhorst to Com
bat on April 30.
NEW YORK, April 17.â The decision of
the Police Commissioners to stop prize
fighting after May 1 probably will have
the effeqt of crowding all fights into the
next dozen days.
McGovern and Tommy Warren, the for
mer featherweight champion, who has
been in retirement for several years, will
fight twenty-five rounds at the Broadway
Athletic Club on Friday night.
Bob Fitzsimmons, Ed Dunkhorst and
the Hercules Athletic Club will post $500
each this week to guarantee the match
between the first-mentioned two, which
will be decided at the Hercules Club
house April 30.
Dal Hawkins, the California light
weight, and Jack Daly of Wilmington,
Del., will fight at the Hercules Athletic
Club next Monday night.

GRAND LODGE KNIGHTS
AND LADIES OF HONOR
Showing Made by Reports of Grand
Officers â Rulers for Next Year
to Be Elected To-Day.
The first session of the Grand Lodge of
the Knights and Ladles of Honor of the
jurisdiction of California was held yester
day in Pythian Castle. Grand Protector
Z. T. Whitten presided and there were
present all the grand officers and dele
gates from the several lodges.
The sessions ln the forenoon and after
noon were taken up ln reading and con
sidering the reports of the grand officers.
That of the grand protector showed that
notwithstanding the increase of assess
ments at the session of the Supreme
Lodge last October the order is in better
condition than at any previous period in
its history. During the year Equity-
Lodge of Sacramento consolidated, with
Harmony Lodge of that city. A Â¦ new
lodge is being organized at Watsonville.
The report of the grand secretary show
ed that there had been seventeen deaths,
the beneficiaries receiving $30,000. There
are now 7&9 members in good standing, a
loss of seventeen by death and of fifty
seven who withdrew on account of the
increased rates of assessment. The Grand
Lodge receved $1385 during the year and
disbursed $919.
The first order of business this morn
ing will be the election of officers. New
opposition "has developed against the
grand protector and A. H. Anderson,, the
vice protector, who wants to advance to
first place. These are Dr. C. C. O'Donnell,
James P. Dockery and J. A. Colquhoun.
For grand secretary Mrs. Antoinette Graff
will oe a candidate against H. W. Qult
zow, ,the incumbent.
WILLING TO SELL
RECREATION PARK
At the regular meeting of the directors
of the Mechanics' Institute last nlyht the
recommendation of Messrs. P. J. Healy,
G. H. Wallis and R. J. Tausslg that the
Folsom-street property be sold was adopt
ed by the board. Real estate men compe
tent to judge appraise the property, now
known as Recreation Park, at $125,000, and
the board is willing to let It go at that fig
ure. It was stated last night that the
property cost 197 ,000 a few years ago but
has since cost the institute nearly $100,000
more. It brings in practically no income.
The institute, on the other hand. Is pay
ing interest on a mortgage of $400,000 on
the pavilion, and wants to be rid of some
of Its burdens.
It was decided to replace the office of
the librarian on the main floor where it
was formerly. Hereafter all books of ref
erence are to be plainly marked "Refer
ence" on the back, po as to avoid confu
sion. The directors are making an effort
to restore the library business to its for
mer basis.

SHIRT ESTABLISHED
SUICIDE'S IDENTITY
Dead Man Discovered to Be Frank
Merwin by the Man Who Gave
Him Article of Apparel.
The mystery which surrounded the man
who was found dead in the lodging house
at 317 Third street last Thursday has
been cleared Up. He was yesterday posi
tively identified as Frank Merwln and the
police and Morgue officials are satisfied.
Detectives AVrenn and Dinan, who were
detailed on the case, succeeded in tracing
his identity by means of the shirt, upon
which deceased had written a facetious
note shortly before he ended his life. Hart
Bouton, superintendent of the La Grand
and White laundries, settled all doubts
yesterday by stating that he knew de
ceased well and had given him the shirt
marked "1001." which was on the body.
Â¦ According to his statement Merwin was
a cook and had signed to act in that ca
pacity on the bark Felton, which was to
sail for Seattle and Nome the day follow
ing his tragic death.
Morgue Surgeon Leland reported to Cor
oner Cole that he had been unable to find
any lesion of the organs that would ac
count for the death of the man. He said
he found some rigidity of the heart, as
though there might have been paralysis of
that organ, but he did not think that the
indications were sufficient to be taken as
the cause of death. City Chemist Charles
L. Morgan, who analyzed the stomach, re
ported that he had failed to find any
traces of poison. He was of the opinion
that if the suicide had taken phosphorus
and strychnine pills traces of the poison
would be found.
FITZGERALD IS ON
TRIAL FOR HIS LIFE
Damaging Testimony Is Given by
George O'Malley, Who Was
Employed by Lennon.
Frwcial DisiiuUc-h to The Call.
REDWOOD CITY. April 17.â A jury was
secured this morning in the Fitzgerald
murder case and the taking of testimony
commenced this afternoon. The first wit
ness called for the prosecution was Coun
ty Surveyor W. B. Gilbert, who identified
and explained a plan of the dwelling
where the homicide occurred.
Sheriff J. H. Mansfield testified as to
certain photographs taken at the same
place about a week after the shooting.
Dr. J. L. Ross, who held an autopsy
upon the body of John Lennon on last
Christmas day. testified as to the cause
of death. Apart from the wound that
caused death he found Lennon to be a
strong, muscular and healthy man about
Fix feet tall and weighing about ISO
pounds. He was not allowed to give his
opinion as to the position of the body
when the wound was received.
George O'Malley was called and testified
that on December 24 last and prior thereto
he had been driving a team of Lennon'a
and living at the latter's house; that Fitz
gerald had also been living there for
some time; that witness arrived at the
place on Christmas eve about 7:30 o'clock,
having returned from a visit to Colma.
and found Lennon lying dead on the floor.
In the dining-room behind the door on the
floor he found an old Springfield rifle
which he identified as the property of
Fitzgerald. He also testified that early in
the morning of the 24th of December he
drove with Fitzgerald from Lennon's
place to Colma to catch the train for San
Francisco. On this trip Fitzgerald said
to witness: "I could put a bullet through
that (referring to Lennon) and noth
ing would be done to me." The examina
tion of the witness was incomplete when
court adjourned and will be resumed to
morrow morning.
