THROWN FROM A BUGGY
AND LOSES HER LIFE
Fatal Accident Occurs to the Wife of
Deputy Assessor Irish of
Placerville.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PLACERVILLE, April 17.â Mrs. C. F.
Irish, wife of Deputy Assessor C F. Irish,
lost her life as the result of an accident
which occurred near Georgetown Sunday
night. Mr. and Mrs. Irish were driving to
their home, and while crossing the bridge
about a half-mile from the house their
horse, which was young and skittish, sud
denly swerved to one side, going off the
bridge. Mr. and Mrs. Irish were both
thrown from the buggy and Mrs. Irish
was thrown head first into a pile of rocks,
being rendered unconscious. Mr. Irish
was badly cut and bruised about the
head and sustained a deep scalp wound,
but he did not itrike hard enough to ren
der him unconscious.
When Mrs. Irish was examined It was
found that her left arm was bruised and
broken In two different places, one side of
her skull was laid bare from a wide gash
and she was completely paralyzed. She
never regained consciousness and died at
9 o'clock this morning.
Mrs. Irish was. prior to her marriage,
one of the best known and most successful
teachers ln the public schools of this
county.
WORK OF SAFE CRACKERS.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MODESTO, April 17.â The fire-proof safe
in the saloon of Henry Vogelman, on
Front street, was blown open some time
Sunday night and about $240 stolen.
The safe-cracker gained entrance- Into
the building through the ventilator in the
cellar. Access into the saloon from the
cellar was easy, as the door opens upward
and was not fastened. A hole three
eights of an inch ln size was bored
through the safe door near the lock, and
powder Inserted and exploded, which blew
the door open. Some of the tools used
were taken from a blacksmith shop ln
town and were left behind. The burglar
also took the content", of the cash regis
ter, securing about $8 or $9. The officers
are working on a clow but no arrests
have been made at this hour.

POPULISTS ARE READY
TO HOLD A CONVENTION
Committee Appointed to Formulate a
Callâ Session to Be Held in This
City on May 1.
There was a large and enthusiastic
meeting of Populists in the assembly
room of the Parrott building last even
ing. It was called at the request of Mr.
Osborne, a national commltteeman, who
was sent out by the leaders of the move
ment to secure delegates from this State
to the convention which will meet ln Cin
cinnati. Horace W. Phllbrook presided
and George A. Clough acted as secretary.
It was unanimously decided to call a
State convention for the Ist day of May,
and as there was a lack of time to hold
primaries or county conventions It was
agreed that the delegates who composed
the convention which met in Sacramento
in IS9S should be called into session again
in the Turk-street Temple on May 1.
A striking feature of last night s meet
ing was the presence of a large number ot
delegates who supported fusion and Ma
guire in IS9B and who are coming out for
united action this year.
A committee consisting of John A. Mil
ler, JameÂ« Taylor Rogers, H. A. Phll
brook. George W. Montelth. T. H. Porter,
Henry C. Greer, G. 11. Hubbell, J. K.
Phillips, C. D. Rodgera and John A.
Prosek was appointed to issue the address
and to formulate a call for the conven
tion.

but -this was -vigorously denied by Lee
Won to the members of tha Lee family,
to which he belongs, after he was re
mdved from the place. HÂ« declared that
after he had been captured he was beaten
and then a knife was drawn across his
throat. The Lee family immediately
raised $200 to prosecute his assailant*, but
after some dickering with the friends of
the latter they agreed that if the man
should survive his injuries he would be
compensated liberally and only ln case
of death should the aid of American law
be Invoked.
The Chinese who wielded the weapon
claims that he did not intend Co kill Lee
Won, but simply desired to mark him for
life. He says his victim suddenly lunged
forward and the blade penetrated farther
than he had desired.
Several nights ago Jue Pon, another out
law from the faction, having ln charge
the shipment of Chinese to Alaska, was
captured and placed in a basement on
Washington street. He was later found
by the officers with hia throat cut. Owing
to the fact that he is a member of the
family that hires the men and who did
the cutting no retribution for his horrible
injuries has been asked.
A TERRIBLE Instance of Chinese
brutality and lawlessness was re
lated to the police in Chinatown
for Investigation last night. Ac
cording to the verified report made to the
officers, the Mongolians have been taking
the law Into their own hands and punish
ing offenders by brutal methods, extend
ing even to attempted murder. Two Ce
lestials who were paid advance money to
join the exodus of fishermen to Alaska
and who squandered the coin over the
fan tan table and then hid until after the
steamers had sailed were captured by
their countrymen and, it is claimed, con
fined in an improvised prison, brutally
beaten and their throats slashed with
razors.
Fifty other Chinese, who also failed to
keep their engagements to their employ
ers, have had rewards placed on their
heads, and circulars announcing this have
been posted in the principal thoroughfares
of the Chinese district. â¢
About three weeks ago Lee Won was
found in the rooms of Jue Bun, Chinese
agent for the Alaska Commercial Com
pany at 711 Commercial street, with a
large gash ln his throat. Friends of Jue
SEXTON ROSENER IS
SUMMONED BY DEATH
SAMUEL. ROSENER, for more than
ten years sexton of Temple Emanu-
El, dfed suddenly yesterday at his
home, 1611 Bush street. He was ill
less than half an hour. When he started
downtown early In the morning he was
apparently in perfect health. : He met
some friends about noontime and com
plained that he was suffering from a se
vere pain in his side, which had come on
rather suddenly and which was giving
him much uneasiness.
He was advised to hasten home and
take a rest. He arrived at his residence
a little after noon and a physician was
summoned. Before the doctors arrived
Rosenor applied a mustard plaster over
the region of his heart, where the pain
seemed most intense.
Complaining to his wife that the plaster
was too strong, he groaned and expired.
Deceased was a native of Germany, 61
years of age. He leaves a widow, four
grown daughters and two grown sons.
He was of a cheery disposition, popu
lar with all who knew him and was held
in high esteem by the congregation of
Temple Emanu-El.

BACHELORDOM
John F. Ward Announces
His Marriage After
Ten Months.
Bride Is Miss Eva Finn, Daughter of
a Wealthy Easterner Who Re
cently Made California
His Home.
ALAMEDA, April 17.â Not until bride
and groom called upon her on Sunday last
to pay their respects did Mrs. Colonel J.
J. Tobin know that her brother. John F.
Ward, had become a benedict. Neither
did any of the many friends of Mr. Ward
flream that he had forsaken single bless
edness for matrimony. Mrs. Colonel
Tobin was taken quite aback herself by
the tardy announcement, and before her
recovery from the shock had confided the
interesting: information to a select few.
That is how the news got out.
To a bachelor's life Mr. Ward's friends
thought he was unalterably wedded.
Nearly 40 when he was a resident of Ala
meda they thought he had passed the
marrying age. No wonder, then, there
will be surprise when for the first time
public announcement is made that in June
last, in San Francisco, Mr. Ward married
Miss Eva Finn, daughter of an Eastern
capitalist who recently came to California
with his family to reside. Mrs. Ward is
handsome and accomplished.
Formerly Mr. Ward was enpraged ln the
real estate business In Alameda and later
was cashier of the now retired Encinal
Bank. At present he is identified with the
Hibernia Bank.

S?ev. C. 0, 32 r own
li/ithdraws &rom SP/s Church
platform. He mapped out a course as a
preacher of the gospel, and after some
years of study in an Eastern seminary re
turned to his native town and was imme
diately seized upon by the trustees of the
then struggling Central Christian Church.
The young preacher's methods attracted
instant attention and thousands flocked
to hear him. His first innovation was to
introduce Shakespeare as a corollary to
the Bible. In his Sunday evening dis
courses he presented with much dramatic
vigor selected readings from the great
tragedies.
He preached the doctrine that many
amusements deemed harmful were inno
cent, and he ran the gamut of the social
diversions of the day. Once he did a
waltz movement in the pulpit. And all
this time the charming soprano's pretty
eyes were fastened on the clever young
preacher. Cupid played havoc in that
choir, and even after the final crash
which left Pastor Davis without a pulpit
the dainty little songbird linked fortunes
with the deposed preacher, and they
were married.
At one time Davis came into the public
eye with a signed story of an interview he
secured with Fay Strahan Moore, who
was in jail for alleged complicity in a
blackmailing robbery of a wealthy New-
York hotel man. She was paid a few
years before to have been engaged to Da
vis during one of his lecture tours in Ore
gon.
Mrs. Davis is the daughter of Mr. and
Mrs. M. H. Kingore. Their home is at 58S
Twentieth street. Her father was for
merly an attorney here, being a law part
ner of Thomas V. Cator.
Mrs. Kingore opnoses her daughter's
divorce, but declares that the troubles, of
the couple may have forced such an Is
sue.

REV. KENNETH DUNCAN
IN THE DOCK AGAIN
Friends Intercede in His Behalf
and He May Be Placed
in Their Care.
w _, ENNETH DUNCAN, the fallen
JLs/ minister, whose career downward
P\^ has been one of the sad features of
z> local criminal annals, has one more
chance, and in the anticipation that it will
THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, APRIL 18, 1900.
materialize to-day Duncan was not given
sentence yesterday on the three . charges
of petty larceny that have been proved
against him. The idea now is to send him
to some private family in Ukiah, where
he can be taken care of and where, ii
there is anything left in him, he can. make
a new start.
His case was called In Judge Conlan's
court yesterday, and when he was told to
stand up to be sentenced Charles Mont
gomery came forward to plead for him.
Montgomery said that at heart Duncan
was a decent, honest man, but that drink
had got tho better of him, and until his
appetite had been controlled there was no
use trying to reform him. .To help. him
to control himself Montgomery proposed
that Duncan be allowed to go to Uklah.
and, while still under the jurisdiction of
the State Prison Commission, that he be
restrained there among friends, who would
do what they could in assisting him to re
form. He asked that Duncan's sentence
be put off for a few months until the re
sult of the experiment could be deter
mined. Montgomery said he had tried be
fore to reform Duncan, but so far he had
failed. He said, however, that he was
willing to try again, and he asked the
court to let him make the attempt. Judge
Conlan set the case over until to-morrow,
when he will announce his decision.
REV. C. 0. BROWN
LEAVES THE PULPIT
Practically Ostracised Since
He Was Mixed Up in the
Overman Scandal.
CHICAGO, April 17.â Rev. C. O. Brown,
who came from San Francisco three years
ago, has resigned as pastor of the Green
street Congregational Church. He gives
no reason, but it is well known that
Brown has been ostracised by the other
Congregational ministers since he visited
San Francisco and confessed the charge
of immorality with a young woman who
was a member of his former California
church.
Brown also withdraws from the min
istry.
HAZEL G. SPEER ACTS
AS HER OWN LAWYER
Files Suit Against Her Husband for
Divorce^ â Other Unhappy Cou
ples in Court.
Stylishly gowned and with a myriad of
diamonds sparkling from ears, throat and
corsage, Hazel G. Speer yesterday ap
peared in the County Clerk's office and in
propria persona filed suit against Andrew
J. Speer for a divorce. In her complaint
Mrs. Speer, after setting forth that she
married her husband in 1593. charges him
with failure to provide. As the proofs are
so plain, she said, she didn't need a law
yer and will prosecute her own action.
Decrees of divorce were granted in the
Superior Court yesterday to Frederick
Kellogg Blue from Gertrude F. Blue on
the ground of desertion; Annie Eriksen
from Bertel Eriksen for desertion; Ada
Carson from Edwin M. Carson for failure
to provide; Mary Murphy from John J.
Murphy for desertion, and Julie Knights
from Joseph V. Knights for desertion.
Suits for divorce have been filed by
Flora A. Maguire against Nicholas J. Ma
guire for cruelty; Harriet L. Skillman
against Oliver E. Skillman, for desertion:
Amanda J. Wardell against Conrad James
"Wardell, for cruelty: Lucinda M. Murphy
against John J. Murphy, for cruelty:
Mary A. E. Falke against William H.
Falke, for failure to provide, and Martha
Jessie Darnley against Charles H. Darn
ley, for failure to provide.

port her.
In truth, after Edwards Davis aban
doned the pulpit In Oakland the pair wore
stranded jn New York. His plans for busi
ness in a secular field went all awry, and
finally the talented and accomplished
young wife put her ability to earning a
living for herself, and she went on the
stage and succeeded so admirably that
now. after a brief stay here with her
mother, she has decided to return to New
York and resume her work on the stage.
Her husband when last heard from was
doing a specialty with a touring minstrel
troupe In Florida. Last fall when Mrs.
Davis left New York her husband was in
Ohio.

which grew out of the clever but erratic
young cleric's career in Oakland. Eel

POLICEMAN IS
INJURED BY A
RUNAWAY HORSE
Captain Thomson Meets
With a Very Serious Ac
cident in the Park.
Thrown to the Ground After Lassoing
the Frightened Animal, Which
Is Finally Captured by
Officer Arellanes.
A runaway horse in the park yesterday
afternoon was the cause of serious In
juries to Captain . Thomson of the park
police, which will result in that popular
officer's retirement from active duty for
some time to come. The accident further
demonstrates the mistake of the Phelan
administration in reducing the number of
patrolmen In a section sadly in need of
more police protection. About 4 o'clock
the animal, which had probably escaped
from some vacant lot near by, â¢ dashed
Into the park road at Sixth avenue. The
brute was not attached to a wagon and
was minus harness, and as he ran along
the road he became suddenly frightened
and tore along at a rapid rate.
The frenzied animal narrowly escaped
several pedestrians on the road, when he
was seen by Captain Thomson, who
chased after him on horseback. Thomson
tried to lasso the horse several times, but
was unsuccessful until he reached the
corner of Haight and Ashbury streets,
when his lariat threw the runaway horse.
The latter tugged hard at the rope and
succeeded in throwing Thomson to the
ground, where he lay unconscious for sev
eral minutes. The patrol wagon was sent
for and Thomson was taken to the Stan
yan-street station, where a cursory exam
ination disclosed that he had sustained
peveral , bruises and was considerably
shaken up. There was also a slight con
cussion of the brain. Thomson was finally
sent to his home to be treated.
In the' meantime the runaway horse had
regained his feet, and with Thomson's
lariat trailing behind him ran at a terrific
gait down the street. Officer Arellanes,
who had come up on the scene by that
time, went after the animal on his horse,
and after a few blocks' ride brought him
to a halt with his lariat.
That no one was injured In the park
during the mad run of the frightened ani
mal was a miracle. There was quite a
number of pleasure seekers almost In the
path of the runaway, but they fortu
nately escaped injury.
This Is the second time within a period
of two months that Thomson has received
injuries in the discharge of his duty. He
was severely hurt in an encounter with a
maddened buffalo several months ago and
had only recently recovered. The fact Is
that the park roads should be better pa
trolled than they are at present and tho
danger from similar accidents would be
reduced to a minimum.
The Philippine Commission Sails.
The Philippine Commission sailed yes
terday on the transport Hancock, taking
with them six new model Remington type
writers, on which will be written all their
official records, orders and documents.
The United States Government has over
3TjOO Remington Typewriters. The British
Government has IuOO. The Governments of
France and Russia have 1500 each. These
four Governments use more Remington
Typewriters, many times more, than of all
other makes combined. The French Gov
ernment railways have just placed an or
; der for 150 Remington*. . ._
DEMOCRATS ARE
PREPARING FOR
CAMPAIGN WORK
Seth Mann Calls a Meeting
of the State Central
Committee.
Members to Assemble May 5 and
Designate Time and Place of Hold
ing State Convention to Choose
National Delegates.
>
Seth Mann, chairman of the Democratic
State Central Committee, yesterday issued
a call directing the whole committee to
meet In this city: on Saturday, May 5. The
executive committee will meet at the Cali
fornia Hotel on Monday, April 30.
The general committee at its meeting
on May 5 will designate the time and
place of holding a State convention to se
lect eighteen delegates to represent Cali
fornia in the National Democratic Con
vention which will assemble at Kansas
City, Mo., on July 4. The committee will
also fix tho apportionment of delegates to
the State convention from the various
counties, and as a basis of representation
will probably take the vote cast for Bryan
in 1596. Should the vote cast for James G.
Magulre in 1858 be taken as the basis the
Democrats of San Francisco would lose
several delegates. The manner of choos
ing delegates to the convention will be an
nounced by the State Central Committee.
It is quite well understood that Jaspar
McDonald and his associates of the Dem
ocratic County Committee will appoint the
delegates to represent San Francisco In
the State convention. There Is no inten
tion on the part of the campaign man
agers to permit the rnnk and file of the
local Democracy to have a voice in the
i selection of representatives. The pro
gramme to appoint was devised to prevent
the voters of the party from expressing
their sense of shame and disgust for Phe
lan's administration. It Is apprehended
by the party manipulators that Demo
cratic voters would take advantage of the
primaries to elect delegates who would
go to Sacramento and openly denounce in
the convention the corrupt methods of th.c
Mayor. Therefore, in order to prevent
discord and strife In the beginning of the
campaign, it has been resolved to take
away from the voters of the party all op
portunity to express a choice in the selec
tion of their representatives. It Is a raw
programme. Seth Mann knows that it is
raw and Judffe Lawlor and others know
If, but they doubtless comprehend that
exigencies of the situation demand a de
parture from party customs and tradi
tions. -

The Republican Alliance of Oakland
Will Be Fully Represented at
the State Convention.
The Republican Alliance of Oakland,
, now in its sixteenth year of active cam
paigning, will participate in the biennial
convention of the California League of
Republican Clubs, which convenes at Los
Angeles the 27th of April, 1900.

Colonel L. L. Bromwell went to Los An
geles Tuesday night and has undertaken
to make all the necessary arrangements
at Los Angeles for the alliance delega

1 board of directors of the bank. The
ground dimensions of the new building
will be: Market street frontage 53 feet,
Geary street frontage 73 feet. The lot is
irregular In shape, and consequently the
depth of the building will vary from 53
feet on the east to 70 feet on the west.
The east line will be about 30 feet west
of the west line of Kearny street. The
structure will be thoroughly fireproof and
modern. To the height of two stories the
material employed will be granite and
above that, sandstone. The general style
will be monumental. The corners, lower
story and the roof line will be ornamen
tal, but the remainder will be plain. Tho
bank will be on the ground floor together
with a store, to be 20 feet wide and the
entire depth from Market to Geary
street. The bank will take the greater
part of the Market street frontage, but
there will be an entrance to the office ele
vators of handsome proportions. The
number of offices will be about 150. The
cost of the building will be about 5300,000.
There were nine architects in competition
for the work and premiums were awarded
to Messrs. Albert Pissis, Swayne & Van
Trees. The work of demolishing the old
buildings and the construction of the new
will begin as soon as all the working
plans are ready. The building will take
about one year to construct.
It was a belief among the Egyptians
that the third finger of the left hand was
connected with the heart by means of a
slender nerve. From that belief came the
custom of wearing the. wedding ring on
that finger.
